1433502410|5.0|In 2001 the English Standard Version of the Bible was released. Its parentage goes back to William Tyndale's 1525 New Testament and the 1611 King James Version. The text of the 1971 Revised Standard Version provides the immediate foundation for this work.<br /><br />Theologian James Packer headed up the translation project, with a team of over a hundred people, including 50 biblical scholars. The work on this project was originally approved of in 1998. A minor revision of the 2001 edition occurred in 2007.<br /><br />As to the translation philosophy, there are two main methods, with various translations lining up along a spectrum. On the one side are formal equivalence, or word-for-word, translations. On the other are functional or dynamic equivalence, or thought-for-thought, translations. Further beyond the thought-for-thought translations are paraphrases, such as the Living Bible.<br /><br />The ESV falls into the formal equivalence camp. As stated in the Preface, the \\"ESV is an `essentially literal' translation that seeks as far as possible to capture the precise wording of the original text and the personal style of each Bible writer. As such, its emphasis is on `word-for-word' correspondence, at the same time taking into account differences of grammar, syntax, and idiom between current literary English and the original languages.\\"<br /><br />Suffice it to say that there are strengths and weaknesses to both of the major approaches. I tend to prefer formal equivalence translations, which is one of the reasons why I appreciate the ESV.<br /><br />The ESV Study Bible appeared in 2008. Leading scholars involved in the overall project include Packer and Wayne Grudem. Old Testament experts include Gordon Wenham, Paul House, John Oswalt, T. Desmond Alexander, Iain Proven and Duane Garrett.<br /><br />New Testament scholars include Thomas Schreiner, Frank Thielman, Andreas Kostenberger, Scott Hafemann, Grant Osborne and Robert Yarbrough. Those even remotely familiar with theology and biblical studies will of course recognise most of these names. Some 95 scholars in total went into the work of this Study Bible.<br /><br />As to the contents of the Study bible, there more than 50 major articles dealing with key theological and biblical topics. There are 20,000 notes which run throughout the Bible, along with 80,000 cross-references. Some 200 maps are included as well.<br /><br />There are also over 200 charts and timelines. In addition there are 40 illustrations. All up this volume comprises some 2 million words stretched over 2,752 pages. Thus this is an important and detailed theological reference work. And given that those who purchase this work also get free access to the online Study Bible, this really is a complete package.<br /><br />But why study Bibles? Why are they important? Those who have a solid theological reference library at home - or access to local public theological libraries - may well have less need for such a study tool. Those with a good supply of commentaries, Bible dictionaries, Bible atlases, and various reference works, will be well-supplied in this regard.<br /><br />But most Christians do not have a large and substantial reference library. Thus a tool like this becomes quite important. In one volume all these various tools are handily combined. One really can stay with this single volume and learn so much about background issues, historical and cultural matters, theological concerns, and so on.<br /><br />I am not arguing that Christians should rely on just one work, and I am a firm believer in the need for all followers of Christ to build up at least a small personal library of some of the more important biblical reference works. But in a massive work like this, those with limited funds or limited shelf space will really have a very good start to biblical knowledge and understanding with this new Bible.<br /><br />Consider just one example of how this volume actually works. The introduction to the bookof 2 Corinthians runs to four pages. All the key issues are nicely addressed. The author, date, and place of writing are discussed, as are the background, occasion and purpose of the epistle.<br /><br />A timeline of Paul and is ministry is also provided. A colour map showing the setting of the letter is included, as is listing of key themes and emphases. Finally a detailed outline of the epistle is provided. While a helpful critical commentary may spend 50 pages or more on such background issues, these four pages give the reader a very good introduction and feel for the letter.<br /><br />There have of course been many good study Bibles produced over the years, such as the Harper Study Bible or the NIV Study Bible. Some study Bibles come from a rather narrow or limited theological perspective, such as the Reformation Study Bible. This one offers more room to move, while remaining firmly in the conservative, evangelical camp.<br /><br />The ESV Study Bible is certainly among the newest, most comprehensive, and most helpful. Those looking for a recent word-for-word translation, coupled with a great set of theological reference helps and tools will find this volume to be an excellent choice. I am happy to recommend it.and place of writing are discussed, as are the background, occasion and purpose of the epistle.    A timeline of Paul and is ministry is also provided. 0316098337|4.0|It is a profoundly moving story.<br /><br />I think one of the reasons this novel has struck a chord with readers isn't just because of the subject matter. Sure, on the surface, it's a harrowing tale. That in itself can be enough to attract a certain following. But the novel is never lurid, never exploitative, and that helped me to keep reading, because what lay on the page was practically all I could manage. Harder details would have meant a reading I might not have been able to finish.<br /><br />And although it does strike at the core of our contemporary internet sensibilities -- in an age of 24/7 news that crosses continents in seconds, bombarded as we are by serial killers, kidnappers and other sordid predators -- that also isn't why the novel has a zeitgeist-like quality to it. After all, in a sense we're already familiar with \\"Room\\". We already know the central conflict of the story going in, even if we don't quite know how this particular story will end. And we see the same conflict in some fashion every day reported by every hard news outlet. We're jaded.<br /><br />No, what makes \\"Room\\", by Emma Donoghue, a special book is the voice of little-boy Jack, who could never be jaded, his mother does such a superb job of protecting him. Seen through his eyes, this devastating novel is virtually halo'd by his innocence, his limited understanding of the circumstances in which he lives. His mother's rapes are an act unseen but still heard, the creak of bedsprings counted, reaching a different total each time, and which he stores away for future reference and comparison. One night it's 257, another night 194. Young boy puzzlement abounds.<br /><br />It's all he knows. Room is all he knows. And moments like the bedsprings, where we know what's happening (and are forced to imagine it because of Jack's limited abilities to absorb and describe his world), pepper the novel and tug our heartstrings. That we're forced to infer such horrible moments creates a brand-new poignancy that I, personally, had never felt before.<br /><br />I don't know that the novel deserves the heaps of praise it's been given. I very much enjoyed it, but I'm not sure it's Man Booker Prize good, an award for which it's been shortlisted. Sure, to narrate an entire novel through the eyes and ears of a 5-year-old is quite an achievement. But at the same time, I can find moments throughout the novel where the voice seemed a bit inauthentic, too cloying, too sweet, a little eye-rolling.<br /><br />Certainly the gravitas one might expect from a serious work of literature is, if not completely missing, less weighty than it should be. But that, also, might be attributed to the narration. How does one convey \\"serious lit\\" through the voice of a toddler, a voice that must be told through very simple language? There are no big words here, just made up ones.<br /><br />Overall, though, \\"Room\\" is a wonderful achievement, written beautifully. If I felt a bit manipulated by some of Jack's slightly too sugar-sweet toddler-isms like \\"meltedy spoon\\" or \\"waterfalling\\" milk, I can forgive the story, forgive the writer, and of course forgive Jack's achingly beautiful voice at the center of the novel.<br /><br />Strongly recommended.ver felt before. 0312600844|3.0|I expected to be dazzled by \\"Freedom.\\" I expected to be provoked to consider the nature of Freedom as a central tenet of American Identity. I expected to be jealous of the writer who has been anointed as one of the great hopes for the modern American novel.  I have avoided The Corrections for a decade, I felt I needed to attack this new book to understand why his books are critically lauded and sell so well.    I wasn't dazzled. I wasn't wiser about the American psyche and moral fabric in the early 21st Century, I wasn't even jealous.      Much to my surprise, I was entertained.      I was entertained even as I doubted the credibility of the characters and the situations. Walter as the iconic good man is also the iconic angry man? Walter's involvement with the Cerulean Mountain Trust and obsession with population growth? Lalitha? The anti-seductress seductress who came to life and couldn't disappear from the scene too soon. What was the whole Joey/Jenna dance about and why did it take up so much of the book? Joey as a 19 year-old meandering through the arms procurement underworld in Poland and Paraguay? Why was this whole sub-plot a part of the book? These flaws, or perhaps failed attempts at satire were subsumed by my pleasure at recognizing the personalities and internal conflicts as being true to life.  I was especially seduced by Patty's voice in the sections of the novel that she narrates -- I would have enjoyed the book even more if it hadn't strayed from her point of view and the story of her marriage.    Despite incongruities of character and absurdities of plot, it is only when Franzen pulls back from the voyeuristic glee of looking closely at flawed, and recognizable people, to shift the novel to the level of social documentary and criticism, that the book truly fails. He is not a novelist of ideas and his lack of philosophical grounding, his need to for detachment and critical distance sabotages the emotional resonance that he has built through characterdevelopment.  In the end, Freedom does not pull the reader towards a greater understanding of what Freedom is in modern America, nor even understand whether it is a state of grace or an unattainable chimera. By grasping for a greater canvas, Franzen diminishes his book and obscures the insights a more focused and less grandiose examination of a modern family could have provided.

1594202842|5.0|Amy Chua's book is a heartwarming and funny memoir of extreme parenting.  It's also incredibly well-written, which is why I gave it the highest rating; every single page reads easily.     Those who give the it a 1-star rating either haven't read the book--the Wall Street Journal excerpt was not completely representative--or have little appreciation for people who do things differently.  Chua's results are hard to argue with: smart, happy, successful daughters.      But the book isn't a regurgitation of the perfect parenting style; it's a story about the obvious merits of the \\"Chinese style,\\" but also a story about how that parenting style required little differences with a different child.  It's a meditation on how tragedy can affect our worldview and shake our confidence.  And, like I said, it's really, really funny.      Those Western parents who expect to be told that they're terrible parents should read the book--it's not about that.  Those \\"Chinese\\" parents who expect to hear that their methods are the best should read the book, too--it's also not about that.  It's just one mother's story.  People should read and review it with that in mind, not criticize the book because they disagree with the author's parenting style--or worse, criticize the book based a short excerpt in the Wall Street Journal.

1400052173|3.0|I started out really liking this book.  Although I first heard of HeLa cells (as I vaguely recall we were told she was \\"Helen Lane\\")  back in med school in the 70's (not so far from the Lacks' family home), I really didn't know that much about them, even that I had benefited from them when I joined the lines for the new polio vaccine in the 50's.  So I liked the scientific history.  The basic story of Henrietta and the sociology of race and medicine back then was also interesting.   BUT, the book devolved into a bloated history of the tenacious efforts of the author to get close enough to the family to get their stories, as well as their various stories.  Some of it was interesting, some was simply TMI.  And, although those stories shed some light on the consequences of the \\"medical apartheid\\" of the 50's, 60's, 70's etc., they also showed the family as rather dysfunctional and apparently greedy in a selfish sounding way, making one wonder about their claims for the profits that came not only from their mother's cancerous cells, but the dogged efforts of scientists to use them for the benefit of others (and the pioneers did it with no thought of monetary reward).  Additionally, although there were more acknowledgments than in 10 average nonfiction books, with multiply redundant readers and fact checkers, some statements crept in that were either scientifically/historically inaccurate, or simply conjectures.  These seemed to serve a polemic purpose that didn't fit in with an accurate history, and weren't really necessary; there was already plenty of info in the book about how badly poor black people were treated by society, including academic medicine.  For those who care, I'm referring in part to Henrietta's mentally challenged/damaged daughter (and who gave her congenital syphilis, the evil, white medical establishment?), who, according to the official at the state hospital, probably? (not certainly) underwent pneumoencephalography, which the author describes as abarbaric study involving drilling holes in the skull.   Yes, the procedure started that way in the early century, but by the time the daughter might have been studied, it was routinely done via a lumbar puncture.   It was not a benign test, but there were no CT or MRI scans in those days, and it was also done on patients who weren't poor or black or confined to awful state institutions.  (I don't discount that whoever had it at that institution probably didn't give informed consent.)  Soon thereafter, the author described daughter Deborah's hives and erratic behavior as likely related to uncontrolled diabetes and hypertension.  I've seen plenty of that, and that's not a conclusion I would jump to.   And what does all that have to do with the primary story?  The bulk of the second half of the book was the bonding of the odd couple Rebecca and Deborah, not Henrietta and HeLa cells.  These may be minor points, but for me they detracted from the overall impact of the story, and I couldn't help but wonder if there were other questionable statements in the book, even if I didn't have the knowledge to question them.    The author stated that the book was edited down considerably.  Not enough, IMO.

That said, I believe the author has been wrongfully attacked as \\"racist\\" in her use of the terms \\"chinese\\" and \\"western\\" and because people do not agree with the way she raised her children.  This is the Mommy Wars rearing its ugly head again.  Come on people!  This is a MEMOIR, not a how-to manual and the author makes it clear she uses the terminology loosely and that she is not referring to all chinese or western parents.  In fact, the author changes her perspective on parenting (or gets her comeuppance, as she puts it) toward the end of the book.  Admittedly, the author does invite some level of criticism by writing this book in a way that seems to be an attack on western parents.  However, I think people should read the book before reviewing it.  This book should be read as a memoir, not a parenting guide.

0316098337|5.0|Room by Emma Donoghue is a fascinating fictional take at a story that compelled the nation in 2009. Jaycee Dugard was kidnapped and held hostage in the kidnapper's backyard for eighteen years, and when she was found, she had two children. In Donoghue's version, a five year old boy named Jack has lived every single moment of his entire life in a small twelve-by-twelve room, seeing no one other than his mother and \\"Old Nick\\" who visits her nightly. Ma and Jack fill their days with Phys Ed routines that consist of running around the room in circles and bouncing on the bed to keep up their muscles, eating carefully portioned meals, watching an hour of TV a day, and reading their nine books. He knows every story and song his mother can remember, but he believes that everything he sees on TV is make-believe because there is nothing outside of Room. Ma shatters his illusions by telling him the true story of how she came to live in Room so that they can come up with a plan for escape, but how will a little boy who has known nothing else react to the Outside? Donoghue does a remarkable job of telling the story through Jack's eyes. He doesn't always understand what he is seeing, but his narration makes actions clear to the reader, a careful balance she does very well. Readers can't help but empathize with this little boy's fears and desire to be safe again, as well as with his mother's need to reclaim her old life. Donoghue's writing reads like nonfiction; it rings so true to the heart, it's almost painful to keep reading. This book definitely earned its title as one of the best books of 2010.

B0042RJWTC|5.0|This review is on the four individually marketed seasons, not the boxed set.    I thoroughly enjoyed this series and wished it had continued on through Edward's and Mary's reigns.  (There are many films on Elizabeth's tenure as queen.)  Excellent costuming, fine performances, and close enough to historical fact to make it a good teaching tool (although not for high school given the nudity and excessive, if not historically inaccurate, use of the \\"F\\" word, particularly in the early episodes).  It was a cruel and despotic era, and the series well depicts it within the confines of what is, despite the series' length, a short period of time.  Jonathan Rhys-Meyers was incredibly good.  Hard to believe he was the same man who portrayed Elvis Presley.    Those who criticize the series for historical inaccuracies all have valid points.  As a historian, I can tell you that the mounting of the cannon depicted during the siege of Boulogne was absurd.  Some pieces recoiled; others didn't.  They were all mounted on an uphill slope, which means that recoil from firing round one would have sent them careening back down the slope, requiring hours of struggle to remount them for - well, one more shot that would have repeated the process.  The proper procedure would have been to construct an upward slope behind the piece so it rolled back into position after firing.  I was also annoyed that, given that the aged Henry VIII's obese form is so familiar, the film makers did not gradually fatten Rhys-Meyers.  The 1970 BBC series Six Wives of Henry VIII did an effective job of growing actor Keith Michell from a lean, mean lovin' machine to a candidate for a fat farm.  On the other hand, if you want to teach a non-historian why America's founding fathers insisted their new government have no established religion, have them watch The Tudors.    Of course, no serious historian would tap Hollywood and the like as a valid source.  Gods and Generals, for example, made a strong effort to be a historically accurate film but insufficient effort to be a good film.  Glory, on the other hand, presented a variety of historical errors, but it was an excellent film.  All historically-based movies make factual errors, some through ignorance, others in the interest of story-telling, and some, especially old Hollywood films, are a joke (e.g., They Died with their Boots On or Santa Fe Trail).  As long as film makers make a reasonable effort toward accuracy, I am not overly concerned.  Historical drama can generate interest in a period or event and prompt the viewer to further study, as The Tudors did for me.    BUT WHY WASN'T THE SERIES RELEASED IN BLU-RAY?  We all know it's coming - with three minutes of scenes previously unseen, of course - to lure us into a second purchase.  I rented it from Netflix and will do the same when it comes to blu-ray.  Also, I could never get the closed captioning to function, which I require when actors speak softly, especially in an English accent.  Beware purchasing it if you are hearing impaired.    Otherwise, bravo Showtime!

1416955070|2.0|Lessons from the Urban Fantasy Front Lines<br /><br />Clarissa \\"Clary\\" Fray has a fateful meeting with a strange blonde haired boy, Jace, at the club Pandemonium when Jace and two companions (Isabelle and Alec) kill a demon boy.  Suddenly, her mother disappears, and Clary is being chased by demons.  What is the history of her past and where is her mother?<br /><br />Before this was chosen for my book club, I had heard of Cassandra Clare (Claire) from Harry Potter fandom as the author of some Draco fanfiction and the \\"inventor\\" of the Draco in Leather Pants trope (see TVTropes . Org).  There was also a kerfuffle about plagiarism and then I saw the endorsement from Stephenie Meyer on the cover of the book, but I tried not to let these ruin my impression of the book.  I walked in with an open mind.<br /><br />The very first thing I noticed when reading was how overwritten the book is.  Clare spends far too much time detailing every aspect of the surroundings of her story, only to forget the story itself.  Don't get me wrong: I hate it when authors skimp on description and you go an entire book without knowing if the protagonist has brown hair or lime green or if the car the hero sees is the same one from earlier or just another background detail.  Description is very good and very necessary, but it should never impede the flow of the story.  But throughout the entire story, Clare inserts overlong passages on clothing, description, smarmy dialogue, and, worst of all, clumsily revealed background story.<br /><br />Want a taste?<br /><br />\\"dark red pendant the size of a baby's fist\\" -> What teenager describes accessories in terms of baby's anatomy, particularly teenagers that don't have baby siblings?<br />\\"the taste was sharp and coppery on her tongue like old pennies\\" -> I think someone has been taking the old adage \\"Put your money where your mouth is\\" a wee bit too seriously.<br />\\"said Jace, eating fries with his fingers\\" -> Are there that many other ways to eat fries that we needed to specify how Jace eats his fries?  Does he sometimes snort fries up his nose?  Slurp up fries off his plate like a dog?<br />\\"Beside him stood a girl...under the skirt of her short green dress, her feet were webbed like a frog's\\" -> Huh?  Usually you describe feet being under a skirt if the skirt is nearly floor length.  But if the skirt is short...does that mean the girl is two feet tall??  If so, how does she stand beside a guy that was described as being rather humanlike?<br />\\"her arms [were] aching and stinging, like raw meat\\" -> How did the author know that raw meat ached and stung?  Or does she mean the ARMS were like raw meat?  Dangling adjectives...<br />\\"He laughed soundlessly\\" -> As this wasn't described by the point of view character, this is another impossibility.  \\"Laugh\\" and \\"soundlessly\\" are polar opposites.<br />\\"She felt a bright surge of shame that burst behind her eyelids like a small sun\\" -> Suns don't burst.  Novas burst.  Water balloons that hit people burst.  Cysts burst.  But suns don't burst.  Plus...when did shame start bursting behind eyelids?  I've always felt that shame burns in your stomach or your heart...but then, I'm just a human, what do I know?<br /><br />Lesson 1: Good, clear descriptions, even if they are boring, are much better than the discombobulating, loquacious meanderings of lavender narrative.<br /><br />Most of the dialogue didn't feel natural given the characters and the situations.  The two basic forms of the bad dialogue were: dialogue set up to make a joke and dialogue for the sole purpose of exposition.<br />I like humor.  I like to be able to read a book that doesn't take itself too seriously (even if it is serious) and to be light-hearted and fun.  But, again, humor needs to be used appropriately.  You don't throw humor around everyone and hope it sticks.  That is what I felt Clare did (heck, even her own characters call each other out on it, saying \\"This is hardly the time for idle banter\\" page260).  She put in a lot of so-called \\"humor\\" and hoped that everyone would find it funny.  Most of the time, I didn't.  Most of the time, I would stare at the \\"humorous\\" passage and go, \\"Okay, so what was funny about that?\\"  I particularly didn't like it when the characters started insinuating that they were witty and funny (I am looking at you, Jace) when most of their dialogue was callous and unnecessary.  As if that weren't bad enough, it was also frustrating how all the characters were the same type of funny (most often, the sarcastic funny).  How did five different teenagers end up with the same style of humor when all of them were raised slightly differently?<br /><br />Lesson 2: Forced humor will make your readers send your book across the room and into your audience's collective fish tank.<br /><br />The other trouble spot was the revelation of important plot points.  Often times, this was handled rather awkwardly in a long passage where one character talks to another character.  I understand that the audience has to understand what is going on and so does the uninitiated Clary.  However, it seemed some of the things Clary learned went beyond what was needed at that moment, that characters who should know some of this information did too much name dropping/convenient info dumping, or that the character looked exceptionally obtuse and unable to put two and two together<br /><br />A few examples:<br /><br />In the beginning of the book, Jace launches into a demonology lecture as he, Isabelle, and Alec face off with a demon (which is observed by Clary).  Why does he do this?  Everyone that he thinks is in the room knows this?  Oh, yeah, it's for Clary (and the audience's) benefit.<br />Clary must be told that Simon is crushing on her (okay, sorta believable), that Luke is hitting on her mom (OMG, really?!  She couldn't tell Luke was crushing on her mom?  I could believe her being ignorant of someone crushing on her, but she should be able to see this!!), that key players aren't what they think they are (no specifics as it might be a spoiler)...you get my drift.<br /><br />Lesson 3: Reveal plot points in a logical fashion.  Don't treat your audience as if they are idiots and won't recognize that X is telling Y what Y already knows.<br /><br />None of the teenagers acted their age.  If Clary didn't constantly say she was almost 16, I wouldn't have believed she was a teenager at all.  She took things far too much in stride, was far too easy come, easy go, and far more emotionally together than you would expect for a teenager that has never encountered Shadow Hunters before.  About the only time she acted like a teenager was when she got into a hissy fit about leaving for the country on vacation (wow, original!).  In fact, all of the characters acted way older than teenaged.  The way Jace acts is more like a man in his early twenties, not a teenager himself.  Even his comment, \\"I've been killing demons a third of my life\\" (page 256) comes off as something a twenty or thirty or forty something would say, not a teenager.  Plus, if he is 15, that means he's spent a whopping 5 years fighting demons.  Wow, I am so impressed...not.<br /><br />All of the teenagers used strong language, much stronger than I've seen in other books (in addition to milder he** & da**, we have plenty of b*tch, b*****d, p*ss, and God's name).  Okay, yeah, so teens swear just like adults do and yada, yada.  However, even if you take into account that teens swear, the swearing just doesn't feel like how a teenager would swear.  I've read other young adult urban fantasy and some (Vampire Academy, Wicked Lovely) had some strong language, but I never once had trouble believing their age.  Not so here.<br /><br />Lesson 4: Teenagers need to act like teenagers.  If you are writing your characters as 20 year olds, don't try to dress them up in teen clothes and have them say every other sentence how they are a teenager.  Your audience won't be fooled.<br /><br />None of the characters were memorable, and none of the characters made me want to root for them and hope that they get out of the crazy circumstances they go through alive.  Clary felt fairly generic, borderline Mary Sue--typical \\"ordinary\\" girl who finds out A) her guy friend is crushing on her, B) she has superpowers that she can learn in 8.2 seconds, C) she is \\"beautiful all along\\", and D) she ends up being forgiven and right all the time!  Yawn.  Jace was aggravating as a smart mouth womanizer, who actually is just beat up inside from the past (BORING!).  I can't tell you how many times I wanted to smack him until he was unable to string two words together.  Also, really hard to like a guy that goes around sabotaging other people's property (the motorcycles) just because the other people are \\"vampires\\".  Am I really supposed to be rooting FOR this guy or what?  As for Isabelle: what is it with these novels setting up this \\"other girl\\" for the sole reason to hate our protagonist?  Isabelle could have and should have been an interesting character, but instead, she is merely the antagonist to Clary, for the sole reason that every story has to have a \\"bad girl\\" for our female protagonist.  Simon felt like your stereotypical gawky best friend \\"unrequited love\\" guy.  Alec could have been interesting, but instead his story was clumsily told.  About the only characters I liked were Luke, Jocelyn and Magnus Bane (who was exhibiting really creepy behavior to Alec), and all of them are adults and absent throughout much of the book.  Pretty sad.<br /><br />Lesson 5: Make your characters unique, so that someone is interested in their fate instead of going, \\"Where have I see this character before?\\" or \\"Does someone have any arsenic I can borrow?\\"<br /><br />While there were some aspects of the story I liked, I couldn't rid myself of the de ja vu feeling as I was reading this book.  It's probably because the story and world feel so much like the love child of Harry Potter and Star Wars.  Clary \\"being normal\\" then learning she was \\"hidden away\\" because of her talents feels like Harry Potter/Luke Skywalker.  Jace is Draco from fanon and a smarmy Han Solo.  Valentine is a dead ringer for Voldemort.  Hodge felt too much like Albus Dumbledore.  When Clary was studying the picture of her mother, I couldn't help but be reminded of the scene in the movie where Harry Potter saw his parents in the original Order of the Phoenix (I am basing this knowledge on the movies, as I haven't finished the Harry Potter series yet).  And could we PLEASE STOP USING the Romantic Triangle plot line?!?!  PLEASE???<br /><br />Lesson 6: Be creative.  This is your story you are telling, not a paperdoll version of your favorite story(ies).<br /><br />Now I know I've beat on this book pretty hard, but there were *GASP* aspects I liked.  I liked the grandness of the scope.  It seems that Clare is really trying to make her novel more than just a simple Good vs Bad guy.  She is trying to make a message about racism (Demons vs. Downworlders vs Shadow Hunters vs. Mundanes, even if this message gets twisted in ways that aren't exactly flattering to any of our protagonists and reeks of Harry Potter).  Clare's story took place in an interesting world of runes (I thought this was particularly cool), demon hunters, and myths coming alive.  I liked how she avoided over-relying on vampires or the chic Urban Fantasy Motif of the Moment.<br /><br />And, just to give Clare another bone, there are exactly two scenes in this book that made me laugh.  The first is when Simon stumbles upon Jace and Clary kissing:<br /><br />\\"You invited him into bed?\\" Simon demanded, looking shaken.<br />\\"Ridiculous, isn't it?\\" said Jace.  \\"We would never have all fit.\\"<br /><br />The second is when talking about the Mortal Cup:<br /><br />\\"Oh, it's big enough,\\" [Jace] said patronizingly, \\"but somehow I was expecting something...you know.\\"<br />\\"It's the Mortal Cup, Jace, not the Mortal Toilet Bowl,\\" said Isabelle.<br /><br />But two funny lines and some creativity do not make a glowing overall opinion.  I still can't get over the slow, sloppy writing, the excessive humor, the boring characters, and the stolen plots.  Not to mention, this is the only book I've ever read that has almost put me to sleep--in the middle of the day during my lunch hour!!  I think the book is too old for younger teens, too much like Harry Potter for older teens, and too juvenile for adults.  I don't recommend and if I am curious about how this series turns out, I'll hunt down forums or Wikipedia.<br /><br />Brought to you by:<br />*C.S. B003VIW8DK|4.0|I admit I came late to the career of Bryan Ferry. I think it was 1985 when \\"Legend\\" came out and MTV was playing the video for \\"Is Your Love Strong Enough?\\" from the soundtrack. That same year was \\"Slave To Love\\". Ferry registered in my 12 year old mind but didn't stick. It would be 1987's \\"Kiss And Tell\\" from the \\"Bright Lights, Big City\\" soundtrack that finally solidified in my mind who Bryan Ferry was (or so I thought). I bought \\"Street Life\\" in 1989 and I was sorta kinda interested. Then I bought \\"Mamouna\\" in 1994 and it didn't really excite. So, it wasn't until 2002's \\"Frantic\\" that I finally got with the program and began listening to Bryan Ferry and some Roxy Music in earnest. Still, I owned very little of each.<br /><br />Now, in preparation for \\"Olympia\\" I have gone back and bought everything, but I haven't had the chance to listen to it all (I've been behind lately). Still, the show must go on, and I listened to \\"Olympia\\" all week long.<br /><br />The first thing that struck me is that it sounded like early 80's Roxy during the first opening bars of \\"You Can Dance\\". After several listens, and several readings of who participated on this album, I am slightly disappointed. I am giving this three and a half stars, but I rounded up to four because I liked \\"Olympia\\". I am disappointed because with such great talents, including three original Roxy Music members, I felt somewhat underwhelmed. Yes, underwhelmed. I mean, the cover of Traffic's \\"No Face, No Name, No Number\\" was less than spectacular. The meandering, rather aimless seven minute \\"Reason Or Rhyme\\" bogged down the second half of the album. Tim Buckley's \\"Song To The Siren\\". Written in 1967, and appearing on his 1970 \\"Starsailor\\" album. I have actually never heard Tim Buckley's version, but I do have Robert Plant's cover version on his 2002 album \\"Dreamland\\". Robert Plant did an excellent job with it, making it one of my favorites, and now Ferry tries his hand at it. I have to say that Ferry also does an excellent job of it, transforming it into a synth-laden elegant elegy of a song. The melody, and more specifically, the mood, is captured just right by Ferry's vocals and arrangement.<br /><br />\\"Alphaville\\" was also a good track, but \\"BF Bass (Ode To Olympia)\\" felt forced and overblown. \\"Me Oh My\\" is also a fine track. So there's about six good tracks, and of those maybe three of them are really good: \\"Heartache By Numbers\\", \\"Shameless\\" and \\"Song To The Siren\\".<br /><br />Bryan Ferry works with three original Roxy Music members (including Brian Eno), David A. Stewart (of Eurythmics), Rhett Davies (who has produced several Roxy albums and Ferry solo albums), Scissor Sisters, Flea (of Red Hot Chili Peppers) and \\"Olympia\\" still somehow manages to not stack up to 2002's \\"Frantic\\".<br /><br />Don't get me wrong here, I am happy to have a new Ferry album, and for a 65 year old he is still pretty hip and cutting edge. It's just that something feels slightly askew here. Three and a half stars.<br /><br />As I mentioned earlier, I wanted to, but did not have the time to, listen to every Roxy and Ferry album in sequential order to better put \\"Olympia\\" in perspective, but I was unable to do so by the time of this writing. 0451169514|4.0|Stephen King's second longest novel (20 pages shorter than THE STAND) has a great deal going for it. Characters that are deeply believable both as children and adults, tons of commonplace items and images that become the stuff of nightmares, the scariest clown in fiction, and a fictional town with such a deep history that you feel like you've been there more than once.  All that being said, it's not a perfect novel, which is why I waffled on what rating to give it. Part of me wanted to downgrade to a 3.5, part of me wanted to give it a 4.5. I split the difference because ultimately my problems with the book were outweighed by what I enjoyed.<br /><br />King does write kids very well. The pre-teen (barely) protagonists of fully half of the novel talk like real kids and mostly act like real kids; the few times where they might seem just a touch more precocious than they should can be attributed to whatever \\"higher power\\" is pushing them around the chessboard of the book's action.  I genuinely like Stuttering Bill, Richie, Eddie, Mike, Ben and especially Bev; Stan Uris is the only one of The Losers I feel a total disconnect from -- the few scenes from his point of view did nothing to make me feel for him the way I felt for the others.  And King transforms those pre-teens into adults so perfectly that you don't need to suspend disbelief that the kids and the adults are the same characters.  The personalities come through even if some of the tics are gone.<br /><br />Part of the reason the book is so long is the inclusion of the \\"Derry Interludes,\\" compiled by the adult Mike Hanlon as a way of showing just how long-lived It/Pennywise has been around and just how severely influenced the town has been. Most of the interludes were interesting and felt like they could have been stand-alone short stories. One or two of them made the book slow down for me, but not so much that I lost interest in the book.<br /><br />What I found scariest in the book was not the big group battles with It, but in the individual encounters, regardless of the protagonists' ages. The quieter the encounter, the more tense I felt.  This is something of a truism for me with horror fiction (books or movies) -- the bigger the effect, the less scared I am. Darken the room and let things flit around the periphery of my vision, and you've got me hooked.  The good news is, in amongst all the town history and big set pieces, there are plenty of these smaller encounters to keep folks like me on the edge of our seats.<br /><br />Where the book ultimately suffers, for me, is towards the end as the two battles with It overlap. King goes off on a sort of metaphysical tear that I suppose is meant to be Lovecraftian, with talk of the borders of reality and aspects of It and so on. Again, it's the big set-pieces. I had the same problem near the end of THE STAND.  Not that I'm afraid of big ideas about the nature of the universe; quite the opposite. But I did feel in this case it came just a little out of left field and lingered just a little too long, distracting from the final fight.<br /><br />Once he gets that out of his system, the Big Finale tears along like a run down the Colorado River rapids and the ending, to me, is strong and worth reaching.  Two moments made my heart beat faster, and at least two made me tear up (granted, an easy thing to do lately).  I realized as I came to the end of the book that these were characters I'd come to care about, and while I wanted the book to end I didn't necessarily want their stories to end. Which, when you come right down to it, is a good endorsement for a book.ndividual encounters, regardless of the protagonists' ages. The quieter the encounter, the more tense I felt.  This is something of a truism for me with horror fiction (books or movies) -- the bigger the effect, the less scared I am. Darken the room and let things flit around the periphery of my vision, and you've got me hooked.  The good news is, in amongst all the town history and big set pieces, there are plenty of these smaller encounters to keep folks like me on the edge of our seats.    Where the book ultimately suffers, for me, is towards the end as the two battles with It overlap. King goes off on a sort of metaphysical tear that I suppose is meant to be Lovecraftian, with talk of the borders of reality and aspects of It and so on. Again, it's the big set-pieces. I had the same problem near the end of THE STAND.  Not that I'm afraid of big ideas about the nature of the universe; quite the opposite. But I did feel in this case it came just a little out of left field and lingered just a little too long, distracting from the final fight.    Once he gets that out of his system, the Big Finale tears along like a run down the Colorado River rapids and the ending, to me, is strong and worth reaching.  Two moments made my heart beat faster, and at least two made me tear up (granted, an easy thing to do lately).  I realized as I came to the end of the book that these were characters I'd come to care about, and while I wanted the book to end I didn't necessarily want their stories to end. Which, when you come right down to it, is a good endorsement for a book.

1594202842|1.0|As someone who has studied child development, this is a terrible way to raise your children. B000LJAEFG|3.0|A FEW SPOILERS IN THIS    This movie seems to be going in a few different directions without really committing to any of them.    I was hoping and half-expecting, for whatever reason, that it would be a little bit like Altered States, in which a campy, ridiculous and implausible plot provides an excuse for a lot of beautiful, but also ridiculous special effects sequences.  Fun to watch because it's pretty, silly, and mind-expanding in the least scientifically rigorous way possible.  Brainstorm isn't like that.  There are a few sequences that are just as visually impressive as Trumbull's work in 2001, but they are all very short, and are there mainly to serve the plot.  Don't watch it for those, watch Blade Runner or The Fountain or Enter The Void.    So is the plot interesting?  There are a few sequences that are compelling in a way only sci-fi can be: a couple gets back together after viewing recordings of each others' memories; a man has a life-changing revelation after experiencing a recording of sex on repeat; scientists hack in to a manufacturing lab's computer to sabotage the military.  That last one had me wondering if it could have, or had already happened.      But none of those events are central to the movie or are explored for more than a few minutes.  The main plot involves one of the scientists who developed this experience-recording technology trying to play back a subjective recording of another scientist's death.  In the meantime, the military takes over the project.  From there a few key points don't quite make sense: the scientist's boss doesn't want him playing back the tape, because it's immoral, or sacred, or something, yet he's totally willing to let the military convert the project to the purposes of brainwashing through recorded psychotic experiences.  (I think - what the military is trying to do is never fully clear, but it's BAD.)  The military, for some other unexplained reason, doesn't want him playing back the tape either, even though that would probably be a nice way to pay him off.  When the scientist finally does get to experience the whole recording (of course), the movie just ends, and, as with everything else conceptually interesting, you're left wondering what the point was.    This all might have made sense if the point of the movie was character development, but it's not, or not much.  To be fair, the film started out well with this.  There's no spoon-feeding introduction, just a lot of disconnected scenes that gradually let you put together what's happening between the characters.  It's well made and directed, but it feels like the characters were meant to supplement something else that wasn't there either.    I generally am interested in movies that are interesting failures more than movies that are successfully conventional, and some people might enjoy this as an interesting failure.  For me, some scenes really pulled me in, but it just never went all the way towards anything, and I finished watching more because I was curious than because it was exciting.

B00005ABKC|4.0|How do you follow up a classic album like Damn The Torpedos? Tom knew he had to do something special here and after hearing the masterful The Waiting, you understand, he got it. This song is catchy, has a great guitar/organ compound, and is just overall inspired. It's the kinda stuff a classic single is made of. Bringing producer/friend Jimmy Iovine back, the album has that thick and full sound of its predecessor but it doesn't go out of it's way to replicate everything. Hard Promises is its own album for sure. A heavy handed legal battle, a difficult lead up to his breakthrough album, his mother passing away. It was a growing period for sure and this album shows more depth, a more mature writer at work. Coming to terms with his new stardom and saying, \\"ok where do we go from here\\". They pull it off with flyng colors.    After shining on all fronts with The Waiting, the album takes a more slow appraoch with the single and other recognizable track A Woman In Love (It's Not Me). It's sparce verse sections with its more rolling along chorus, full of guitars and some great solid drum fills from Stan Lynch make it a key cut. But on to the unknowns: Nightwatchman a great more groove oriented mid paced tune full of cutting guitar riffs and some good stomping drum/bass work tells of a guy who is good at what he does but maybe wants more out of life. Something Big and Letting You Go are more slow paced songs, Letting You Go being the better of the two, it has an irresistable whooa/oooh sung line, its got more of a powerful feel still being a slow song. King's Road returns the band to that Byrds style guitar sound, almost sounding like a scrapped cut from Torpedos (complete with that classic TP & the Heartbreakrs shaker). One of the great underlooked tracks here is A Thing About You, fast paced, anthemic, chiming guitars and a frantic chorus it kinda comes along and reinvigorates an album that seems to take more of a slow burn approach that another decent cut The Criminal Kind takes) than the tailwind rock of Torpedos. Insider is the other hit off the album, originally written for Stevie Nicks who loved the bands sound, it ended up being a duet song, a slow organ/acoustic driven number that appears on all the best of's. It's a beautiful song that really shows Tom's change in the compisition department.     Hard Promises is a much more mature effort. But a great one all the same, the songs dont have that rocking melodicism that Torpedos have, but a more subdued rock sense. They reveal more of the bands Byrds/Dylan influence than ever before. Some feel this be the Heartbreakers very best record and some might be right. The writing is masterful throughout and it's great Tom sacrificed the angry more determined style on Torpedos for the growing effort of HP. This would be Toms last completly consistant record till Full Moon Fever but like all great songwriters and bands, theres still a handful of worthwhile and semi classic songs throughout.

B000065AI3|4.0|Tom Petty is a real rock n roller first and foremost. The guy is a brilliant songwriter. He truely understands the craft and magic behind sitting down writing a song and recording it with a band, a band of friends you hang with and party with and make music with. When his former band Mudcrutch split up after signing with famed English producer Denny Cordell's (Leon Russel, Procol Harum) indie label Shelter Records in 1974, Tom was recording his debut record with session musicians in Hollywood but decided one day to go home and check on his old friends. Guitarist Mike Campbell and keyboardist/pianist Benmont Tench along with a rhythm section consisting of Stan Lynch on drums and Ron Blair on bass. He realized the huge potential, snagged em, brought em in and cut the first Heartbreakers album released in '76.     Of course Mudcrutch had played some great bar music in the Southeast but for some reason on record it didnt translate so when Petty brought in 2/3ds of that band plus 2 new guys it seemed like something had been missing all along and this record shows it. In 76, Springsteen had just gotten hot with Born To Run and the singer/songwriter rocker idea was there. Petty had that but he also had a great set of songs that like Springsteen, told stories. Slice of life rock n roll stories about friends, relationships, past times, and a sort of epic poetry style that packed the good with the bad. Rockin Around (With You), Anything Thats Rock N Roll and Hometown Blues are decent British influenced punk styled rockers that landed the band huge attention across the pond but it was stand out rock gems like The Wild One Forever, Strangered In The Night, and the brooding truely rocking classic Fooled Again that really make this an inspired record front to back.    The more country oriented Mystery Man show the bands early influences and its the bands first single Breakdown that show that heavy Dylan sound they were flirting with. Breakdown and American Girl went onto become radio favorites but at the time this album was really a cult item. It would take the next 2 years before it went fully discovered (in America). The album is great, it really demonstrated everything Petty and band loved and made it their own. It was like an accidental miracle, finding these guys you love to play with, finding that song and making your first real record and that youthful attitude is all over it. The world is right there infront of us lets take it. Any real rock n roll fan needs this debut from one of the best bands of all time.

B003ZJJFKE|5.0|This CD seems like a swing back to Satriani's earlier work, like Surfing with the Alien and Flying in a Blue Dream. I like it very much.    There is little more that can be said about Satriani which hasn't already been said a thousand times. He is a fantastic guitarist and musician, very creative, great stage presence (check out his DVDs), and so on. The thing which makes him stand out for me is the extreme musicality of his work. Lots of people can shred like a buzzsaw, but Satriani's creations are melodious and deeply musical. I consider him the most melodious of all the shredmeisters.    Much of his music has an anthem-like or heroic quality. It seems to uplift and transport the listener (at least it does that for me - I have even had out-of-body experiences listening to some of his pieces - and, no, NO drugs were involved, in case you wondered). I also do some qigong exercises to certain cuts and find that they accentuate the flow of qi in some ways. Way cool music.    Cut number 7, The Golden Room, reminds me a tiny bit of the style of a band I heard once in the late 70s, called Carmen, that did flamenco music at hard rock volumes, complete with dancers on stage. Very cool. It is a style that lends itself to heavy instrumentation well, and has not been explored as much as other styles (at least as far as I know).      UPDATE: That number 7 cut inspired me to go looking for anything by Carmen, and sure enough, their albums have been re-issued on CD. More unexpected inspiration from Satriani - Thank you, Joe!      Another great release from one of the world's all time great musicians. Thanks, again, Joe. Your work is appreciated more than you know.

B000VMFDW2|4.0|Near the end of the book, Sledge laments his skill as a storyteller, wishing that he had the right words to finish up his details about the battles he fought in the Pacific in the Second World War.  Alas, there is no summation, and this is not a defect in Sledge's narrative skills.  Instead it is a reflection of the reality of modern war.<br /><br />Noncombatants the world over may have known that old lie: \\"Dulce and decorum est pro patria mori.\\"  We have moved away from a glorification of warfare, in part I think because the ambiguity of the major wars we have fought since the Japanese surrender.  Yet we still fit the events of WWII as part of some grand narrative in two parts - a race from Pearl Harbor to Berlin and Tokyo.  Sledge shows the lie and the actual terror of the combatant and the unreality of war.  One of the most telling things for me was that even after the fact and with access to resources he is often unable to exactly figure out where he is in time and space.<br /><br />Sledge lived to tell the tale; through two major battles he avoided the \\"law of averages\\" and missed out on the Purple Heart.  He was one of the lucky ones, but this book is not about him.  This book is about all those who were not a lucky as he was and all the men of the armed forces that found shallow graves on lonely islands in the Pacific.  He writes a fitting eulogy for all those lost men; it is an eulogy too often told.

1599218011|5.0|Exuberance leaps off the pages of Kris Carr's latest book, \\"Crazy Sexy Diet.\\" It's an inspiring diet and lifestyle program that she developed to heal herself of a health imbalance. \\"Imbalance\\" is her elegantly understated way of referring to the nearly two dozen tumors that had developed in her liver and lungs. In facing this cancer, she determined that she would not engage in battle. Rather, she would embark upon the greatest adventure of her life. Such unabashed moxie is seldom seen, but in this book she puts it in the palm of your hands.    In the book's Foreword, Dr. Dean Ornish, (founder and president of the Preventive Medicine Research Institute and Clinical Professor of Medicine at the University of California-San Francisco) refers to Kris' approach as being based not on a fear of dying, but on the joy of living. She abundantly demonstrates that contagious spirit throughout the succeeding chapters.    She begins by giving us a vivid contrast. Prior to her diagnosis, she was a jet-setting starlet, eating to be thin, with a list of medicated and self-medicated symptoms and an atrocious yet mainstream convenience-based diet. Once diagnosed, however, she became a self-healing, reflective, iconoclastic \\"wellness warrior,\\" empowered by her own educated choices, realizing the strength that resides in a simple plant-based diet, nestled within a meditative and thankful lifestyle. As she states it, \\"[I traded] my fast-paced New York City party life for...a simple, nature-filled existence in Woodstock, New York. I exchanged road rage for prayer, fast food for fasting, swapped martinis for organic green drinks and a compassionate vegan diet.\\"    While not everyone can make a physical move as significant as Manhattan-to-Woodstock, each of us can improve other aspects of our lives, which is exactly what Kris demonstrates for us in \\"Crazy Sexy Diet.\\"    It's more than just diet, she is quick to tell us. It's about a fully-integrated and health-supporting lifestyle. Sustainable healthcare is comprised of yoga mats and meditation sessions, in addition to good food.    The book contains many testimonials, which reinforce Kris' approach to life. Each chapter contains a review section, which serves as a checklist to someone adopting her prescription. She has a significant posse of guest contributors, from medical doctors to Wayne Pacelle of the Humane Society of the US, from actress Emily Deschanel to Jivamukti Yoga co-founder Sharon Gannon. Her writing is irreverent and provocative, without sacrificing meaningful information. Not once does she commit the fatal flaw of preachiness.    This last point is important, for the changes she's suggesting are rather wholesale. It would be easy for a writer, having once healed herself, to become dogmatic or heavy-handed. Quite the contrary, she is candid about her own deviations, and encourages a compassionate application that considers not only our own flaws, but the feelings of others. Compassion is one of the key items in her diet, it seems.    In presenting us with the path she's taken, she reminds us that life is a creative endeavor. This is a very important lesson for us all, especially as we see the status quo crumbling before our eyes. It's time to do things differently.    Dr. Ornish states that \\"joy, pleasure, and freedom are sustainable.\\" Kris is a vibrant embodiment of these ideals, and thankfully she's provided a recipe everyone can follow.

B003G4W49C|3.0|Ender's Game is a classic science fiction novel by Orson Scott Card (if you have not heard of him, welcome to science fiction, and don't worry you will have many more books to choose from in a couple of years, once you get to fourth grade and your mom allows you to pick your own books to read). This is the first in a series of books in the same universe that Card has created over the years, and was originally published in 1985. It has held up much better than my now defunct microwave, which was created the same year, and will soon need to be laid to rest. In fact, the version I read was the reprint from 1991 that included an introduction by Card, attesting to the lasting quality of this story. I have often heard about this book, but have never actually picked it up, and I thought it was high time.<br /><br />Card is very imaginative in his world creation, using the familiarity of Earth as a foundation to bring in identification by the reader, with enough imaginative novelty to intensify the curiosity of the reader. One can easily imagine this as Earth's future, or possibly an alternate universe of our Earth. In this universe, technology has advanced and the human race has been previously attacked from space by the buggers, an insect-like race bent toward the obliteration of humanity. Previous attempts to communicate with the buggers have been met with silence, and only the fortunate happenstance of a brilliant war strategist appearing at the right moment saved the human race from complete annihilation during the previous bugger wars. The Earth has created an international stellar armed force that has both the ultimate authority, and the ultimate responsibility of saving mankind.<br /><br />Ender is born with the genetic predisposition to be a genius. He is a Third, a third child allowed to be born where all families are limited by diktat to only two children. He is allowed to be born with the hope that he will be the last great hope of humanity, a brilliant strategist and military commander, able to defeat the buggers in the next anticipated invasion. The story is viewed in the third person, mostly through Ender's perspective, with some exceptions that play a prominent role in revealing important plot developments.<br /><br />On the whole, the plot works well. We begin with Ender as a six-year-old, and watch him develop and grow as a warrior and a leader. We bear witness as he learns about himself and those around him. As the novel progresses, there are surprising plot twists and developments that ultimately add to the excitement, because as clever of a reader as we might be, there are cards that Card has held up his sleeve, to reveal only in due course. (Yes, I realize the that was pun that is groan worthy, but admit it, you laughed a little. Besides, I first buttered you up by calling you clever, didn't I?)<br /><br />One bit of contention that I had that was a large factor for me, was that Card did not demonstrate even an inkling of understanding about children and their development. I realize that the book was supposed to highlight the contrast between Ender being a child, and the adult-like expectations, the very continued existence of humanity that was placed on his little shoulders. I know that some will argue that this was a useful tool in illustration of Ender's predicament. To be able to demonstrate how insane it was to have a child placed in such an adult position having to deal with war and death. However, every child, no matter how much of a genius they are, is going to act like a child much of the time. There are things that are going to be beyond their understanding, because there are aspects of the adult world that just are not understandable by kids. In sum, children, even genius humanity-saving wonder children, are still children and would still develop in the same way children do. In this case, I was never able to see Ender or his compatriots as actual children. They were instead little adults. This really detracted from the story for me, because I was never able to fully buy into the story of a child being thrust into adult responsibilities, because neither Ender nor the other \\"children\\" in the story were actually believable as children. I know that Card has other stories with young protagonists, so I am not sure if he is an author (like many) that cannot write children at all, or if this was just an early novel that he has since learned from, or if he just thought the story really needed an adult-like child to point out some of the atrocities of war. I look forward to reading some of his other books to gain some insight.<br /><br />In the end, I was still able to still enjoy many of the elements of the story and Card's creative world, so I would still recommend reading it. If you can do as I have done, and separate out the enjoyable elements of the book, and still enjoy them even though it is a book about children with no children in it, then you will likely have a good time.

This is the most noticeably repetitive thing, but there are plenty others.  I also believe Dawkins didn't focus enough of this book on scientific arguments. Or, to be more accurate, I believe he spends too much time talking about the evils of religion throughout history. I was glad that he mentioned some previously unheard of things for me (Hell Houses) but, the last hundred pages are so are completely dedicated to bringing up the horrors of religion, and these just aren't Dawkin's specialty. The horrors of religion should be left to Hitchens, and Dawkins should take charge of the science.  All things considered, I was glad I read this book. There are points of it I will read again, mainly the early scientific sections. However, on re-reading, I will skip the last hundred or so pages. There is very little new here, and almost nothing that has not been done better by Hitchens. Granted, this book came out before God Is Not Great, but I still believe Hitchen's descriptions of religious craze are better.

0143116177|5.0|The Ascent of Money was not at all what I expected. I was hoping that this book would be about the how money has evolved over world history. It isn't and I was disappointed. Fergurson focuses mainly on developments in Western Europe and the US in the last few hundred years.    Then, as I read further, I came to the realisation that this right. Over the past few hundred years it was Europe that invented the modern fiscal systems of banks, money lending, bond markets, share markets, insurance, and so on. Before that money was just a commodity used for barter.    But then I came to a greater realisation - something I suspect few people realise - that finance is the invisible but powerful force that guides the fates of economies. Finance is critical to understanding the fates of nations.    For example, the Ascent of Money gives insight into how Britain and Holland largely beat Spain and Portugal as empires by winning the finance arms race. Britain and Holland developed bond markets while the Spanish and Portuguese continued to believe that silver was money. It details how the Rothschild banking family controlled to finances of Europe in the 19th century to the extent that they decided who made war.    Fergurson also gives many examples of how fiscal systems have failed in the past. It struck me here is how much there is to learn from fiscal history. Yet we forget these lessons causing ourselves great harm in the process. We need only look to the global financial crisis to understand this.    So I am glad that this book was not what I expected. It has opened up a part of the world I barely knew existed.

1400052173|5.0|There is a reason why this book is a New York Times Bestseller. Once I was made aware of the popular book I quickly purchased it and began the journey of discovery.  Henrietta Lacks was a 31 yr. old tobacco farmer from Virginia who was admitted into John Hopkins Hospital with cervical cancer.  Unbeknownst to Henrietta, some of her cancerous cells were removed and sent to the research lab.  As it turns out, Henrietta's cells never stopped growing.  It was an astonishing breakthrough in cell research because now scientists have an endless supply of cancerous cells to conduct research and tests.  Although Henrietta died not long after being admitted, her cells, HeLa (the first two letters of her first and last name) continue to grow to this day.<br /><br />Along the way, we meet the doctors, scientists and researchers who knowingly or unwittingly participated in cultivating HeLa into a multimillion dollar industry.  Most of the major strides in cancer research and treatment as well as the Polio vaccine, DNA and genome research can all be traced back to HeLa cells.<br /><br />Through Skloot's own personal story we learn of Henrietta's family and what they had to endure after learning of the existence of Henrietta's cells after 20 years of being kept in the dark.<br /><br />The fact that Rebecca Skloot dedicated 10 years of her life to research and write about Henrietta Lacks, HeLa cells and the Lacks family, should be an indication of just how powerful this story is.  It is one of the best books I've read in a while.  I highly recommend.

0312243022|4.0|Michael Cunningham uses The Hours to demonstrate the wide significance of Mrs. Dalloway and its relationship with three very different women. We begin with what seem like three entirely separate stories set in 1941, 1949, and the late 1900s. The main characters of the stories of these times are, respectively, Virginia Woolf, an author struggling to write Mrs. Dalloway; Laura Brown, a housewife; and Clarissa, the friend of a dying writer.    (WARNING: SPOILERS in this paragraph.) For the Cunningham's Virignia Woolf, Mrs. Dalloway is a manifestation of her own insecurities and a foreshadowing of her end. For example, Woolf is insecure about her ability to manage a household: She feels as if she is always sneaking around behind her servant's back and asks herself why she cannot \\"command [her servant's] respect and love,\\" so she decides to make Clarissa Dalloway the opposite of herself in this respect (Page 87). Clarissa will be imbued with great skill with her servants.  Woolf is also forever dissatisfied with her own writing:  \\"One always has a better book in one's mind than one can manage to get onto paper,\\" she thinks, and this self-critical perfectionism contributes to her mental illness (69): She is always waiting for frightening headaches, which coincide with periods of extreme mental instability and the hearing of imaginary voices, to descend upon her. Her illness perhaps appears in Mrs. Dalloway as the illness of Septimus Smith, the mad visionary who, like Woolf when she is ill, is trapped in his own world. Like Cunningham's Woolf, Smith commits suicide.    (WARNING: SPOILERS in this paragraph.) While Mrs. Dalloway is a vehicle for Virginia Woolf to muse on her shortcomings with servants and her mental illness, for Laura Brown Mrs. Dalloway is a parallel to Laura's own life. Brown is an unremarkable woman who had the apparent good fortune of being proposed to by a capable, handsome young man in her community. She is pregnant with their second child, but she admits to feeling \\"unmoored\\" when she is alone with her son, as if she's simply forgotten how to be a mother in those moments (47). Her insecurities echo Woolf's Clarissa's insecurity about her decision to enter into a respectable, unremarkable marriage. But Brown, like Woolf's party-planning Clarissa, is planning something: Brown is making a birthday cake for her husband. Like Woolf's Clarissa, Brown worries at one point that her plan is failing before realizing that everything will be alright after all: Just as the party will go on because Ralph Lyon has beat back the curtain, a new cake can be made. Like Clarissa stepping away from her party to think about Septimus, Laura steps away from her cake to read the passage of Mrs. Dalloway in which Clarissa muses on Septimus's death. As it is for Woolf's Clarissa, Laura's stepping away is a revelatory experience because she realizes on her excursion that she \\"loves life...loves it hopelessly, at least at certain moments\\" (152). Though she feels consoled to know that she can choose to die, she will not die like Septimus.    (WARNING: SPOILERS in this paragraph.) As for Cunningham's Clarissa, although she does not come into direct contact with Mrs. Dalloway aside from being nicknamed \\"Mrs. D\\" and knowing the son of Laura Brown, reader of Mrs. Dalloway, Cunningham's Clarissa is revealed to be a modern version of the original Mrs. Dalloway. She faces the same questions and people as the original woman, but in a different time. For example, she questions the worth of her life, but instead of questioning herself as she mends a dress, she questions herself as she passes by a movie star's trailer, musing that though she will die and be forgotten, Meryl Streep or Susan Sarandon will survive as influences on popular culture. In a way, Woolf's Clarissa and Cunningham's Clarissa know the same people, but these people are updated in Cunningham's book: Peter Walsh, the critical friend and former romantic interest, for example, becomes the man named Richard, dying of a modern disease, AIDS, in Cunningham's book. Doris Kilman, the German woman who spends too much time (for Clarissa's taste) around her daughter Elizabeth, becomes Mary Krull, the too-radical feminist who spends too much time around Clarissa's daughter Julia.    Stylistically, The Hours is much more accessible than Mrs. Dalloway. Cunningham does not attempt to mimic Woolf's long, sometimes meandering (albeit pleasantly rhythmic) sentences, so his novel is considerably easier to read. He also abandons Woolf's use of free indirect discourse: His chapters are clearly labeled \\"Mrs. Woolf,\\" \\"Mrs. Brown,\\" or \\"Mrs. Dalloway,\\" at the top of each page. Occasionally, he changes viewpoints (to Mary Krull, for example) but he indicates these changes clearly (\\"Fool, Mary thinks\\") so there is no room for confusion (160).     The only irritating aspect of The Hours remains Cunningham's resolute refusal to expound on why \\"[a]t this moment, it is enough\\" (185). Readers are never given a complete explanation as to why exactly Woolf's Clarissa, Cunningham's Clarissa, and Laura Brown find their unexceptional lives worth living when the most remarkable of them all, Virginia Woolf herself, chooses to die. Are we just to accept the simplistic and coldly scientific explanation of Virginia being mentally ill? This missing explanation is the sole flaw of a book that otherwise provides an interesting perspective on the uncontrollable, potentially disappointing nature of creativity and the timeless relevancy of Mrs. Dalloway. It is a worthy tribute to the still very alive genius of Virginia Woolf.

He combines a sense of humor with some serious, serious scientific factual research. So as heavy as the material may be, it's easily absorbale because he adds some simple, clever banter to bring your brain back down from \\"huh?\\" And I love the science stuff. His experiences and findings are cool enough to read the book even if you dont want to try for the results that could come from it. He's seemingly spent almost a lifetime gathering all of this. B003L77TZI|3.0|My musical tastes are very diverse.  The rendition of Johann Pachebel's Canon in D major can move me to tears, so soul stirring is the classical piece. But I also like loud music with copious amounts of drive bass and electric guitar riffs that could peel the paint off the side of a barn, hence my unwavering attraction to Def Leppard's seminal 1987 album Hysteria.<br /><br />So this diversity in musical styles explains why I can listen to The Band Perry one minute and totally switch musical tracks and drive into Katy Perry's latest release Teenage Dream (2010).  Yes, I said Katy Perry.  I know, I know, she vocal abilities leave a lot to be desired as does her grasp on the world in general if her interviews are not just a façade--this woman is a bit of a dullard--but the production value on this album--at least the first half--is first rate and I enjoyed it.<br /><br />Katy Perry--real name Katheryn Elizabeth Hudson--grew up singing gospel music and after dappling in the genre and seeing limited commercial success decided to give it up and go a different way, releasing I Kissed a Girl in 2008.  The rest as they say is history.  Katy's sophomore album Teenage Dream was released in August 2010 and debuted at No.1 on the Billboard 200 music chart.  The CD has since spawned the singles California Girls (track No. 3), Teenage Dream (track No.1), and Firework (track No.4) , all of which have made it to No.1 on the Billboard Hot 100 music chart and been big hit worldwide.<br /><br />As I mentioned above, I thoroughly enjoyed the first half of Teenage Dream, excluding Firework, which I found almost unbearable to listen to, lyrically insufferable and naive.  To wit:<br /><br />Baby you're a firework<br />Come on let your colors burst<br />Make 'em go \\"Oh, oh, oh!\\"<br />You're gunna leave 'em fallin' down-own-own<br /><br />Seriously?!  It reads like something my 11-yearold daughter would write!<br /><br />Why you might ask?  Because of the driving beats, I would answer.  I love the thick bass, heavy guitar riffs and general loudness of the first half of the album, I can't help but be drawn in by the upbeat and danceable tempo of Teenage Dream, track No.2 Last Friday Night (T.G.I.F.), California Girls feathering Snoop Doggy Dogg , and track 5 Peacock (an homage to My Humps by the Black Eyed Peas). For the most part these opening tracks are upbeat and are endowed fairly catchy lyrics that must have been simple to write, there is a repetitive nature to them that make them easy to remember and playback in your head.  Musically, the tracks are hip, driven and perfect for dancing, but vocally, they are just so-so.<br /><br />If the first half of Teenage Dream was shot through with sunshine, though I have to wonder what Snoop \\"D-O-double G and I'm here to put this d_ck on you\\" Dogg is doing here pitching a decided rated PG-rated rap; \\"squeeze her buns\\" are you serious Snoop?  One would have to guess that Katy and team decided they needed to give the CD a bit of edginess that they thought a muted Snoop Doggy Dogg could only bring.  Judging from the success of the single perhaps they were right, but what did Snoop get from the deal?<br /><br />The back half--with the lone exception of track No.7, The One That Got Away--is almost all darkness and just plain messy and not at all a joy to listen to.  Let's be honest Ms. Perry vocal talent is somewhat lacking, and without extensive instrumentation to cover it up, there is nothing laudable about it.  This is a really evident on track No.9 Who Am I Living For wherein she screams the bridge and her voice is so strained its almost painful to listen to.<br /><br />Ms. Perry's one attempt at a ballad, track No.12 Not Like The Movies is an abysmal mess where she tries to make for her decided lack of vocal acumen by faux yodeling, shortening notes that should have been drawn out; the aural effect was sad to say the least.<br /><br />Teenage Dream includes two hidden tracks; California Gurls (Passion Pit Main Mix)--skip it, it is really bad, unless you're into techno-infused European nightclub music; and Teenage Dream (Kaskade Club Mix), another mess that embarrasses the slick production and solid sound of the original.<br /><br />Katy Perry is a co_k tease; indeed in her own words she admitted that she prefers to play the role of the sexual tease, but nothing more.  So while you will find a few expletives on Teenage Dream--Circle The Drain (track No.6)--is loaded with F-bombs, there is little else here but harmless sexual innuendo; who can miss the underlying message in Peacock?  When she is living in that persona, Katy Perry's music is fun, hip, energetic, but when she attempts to get serious about life, her music falls flat because she is not clever enough, or wise enough to lyrically pull it off. That is why the second half of Teenage Dream did not work for me.uropean nightclub music; and Teenage Dream (Kaskade Club Mix), another mess that embarrasses the slick production and solid sound of the original.
