0307455874|4.0|This is a 'reactionary' work, an adequate word to use, the major theme is Obama proposing an alternative to the Reagan revolution. It's certainly not limited to that. I suppose one could say the anti-Reagan arguments in 'The Audacity of Hope' are wishful thinking, or that they are at the base root of Obama's thinking, or that the arguments are simply the enforcement of political correctness - a rationalisation, an explanation for his beliefs after the fact, however, I'm not qualified to make that claim. For the most part it seems Obama argues with 'Reagan', not with name calling, not with insults, not with intimidation, but with facts and reason.<br /><br />I thought for sure I was going to give this 3 stars - it isn't till page 40 that Obama says something interesting. Then I thought, I'm not going to give it 5 stars, because it's not really about anything. By the end of The Audacity of Hope the reader understands this work is about everything. Obama's theory of everything. I'm not going to give the work 5 stars. . . because I wonder if President Obama will be judged one day. My own reputation is at stake. If it can be proposed for George Bush, it can be proposed for another president.<br /><br />Constantly and on end Obama takes both sides of every issue. Thought I'd use the phrase, 'The Audacity of BS' in here somewhere. I began to wonder if he has a personality disorder, along the lines of say, PHD Sheldon Cooper, the fictional character on the show The Big Bang Theory. He has a single mindedness to be oppositional. Perhaps it's what we need in a president. A professional reviewer notes that Obama examines both the liberal and conservative position, noting positive points of each, and then explains why he prefers the liberal position.<br /><br />President Obama has an interesting mind. His views seem based on history. Obama gives us a very good historical background on each issue he presents. In this he reminds me of Pat Buchanan. Buchanan, apparently, paid attention to what was going on, as it happened, during American history, took it seriously, not just as a sound bite, and he uses this knowledge during debate. He's a very good debater. Do I believe, as a youngster, as these events were unfolding, Obama looked upon them with an objective viewpoint? No I don't. Barack Obama has been busy since then until his election to the Senate. Genuinely Obama has come to understand the past as it's unfolded. He made the most of someone else's opportunity. Obama presents a very fair, and to my mind, very thorough examination of the background history of each issue. It borders on incredible - take the observation how you like. I accept all of Obama's history lessons with the exception of, for reasons that may become clear later in the review, Obama's insinuation that the coarsening of political discourse in this country can be blamed on Ronald Reagan - tho perhaps this one overemphasis is meant to show he Can be unequivocal? He's on your side. .<br /><br />I definitely recommend the Audacity of Hope for anyone wanting to understand America. There are flaws in the book and Obama writes almost as if he's a student determined to show what he knows. One reviewer used the word 'thesis' to describe it. They say a lesson well taught is a lesson well learned. These observations contradict the idea this is some kind of book-by-committee. To be honest, I'm surprised Barack Obama, someone who's not a professional writer, is the best writer I've ever read. George Will notes that Obama may be the best writer-president we've had.<br /><br />The Chapter on Faith, I thought, This would surely be an insult to my intelligence. Little did I know it would be a personal insult. Near the beginning Obama writes, \\"America's religious impulse helped foster a degree of religious entrepreneurship unmatched elsewhere in the industrialized world. Pushed out of sight but still throbbing with vitality through the heartland and the bible belt, a parallel universe emerged. . .\\" I felt that was uncalled for. Obama brings up angry and fiery Alan Keyes in this chapter, the opponent he defeated in his senate race. I didn't realize Keys so directly represented me - sure, I thought it a little odd how 'white' his viewpoints were. It's good to know in some districts I'd be worth 27% of the vote.<br /><br />Alan Keyes is one of my favorite politicians. I didn't realize he was a 'joke'. A US Senator is not ignorant, but frankly I thought Barack Obama was an idiot or at least pretty ignorant about half the world until the day he was forced to debate Alan Keyes. I thought an 'education' was the very reason Keyes was recruited to oppose him. I'm sad to say Obama brushes off Keyes pretty easily in this (his) book. This chapter brought up the possibility, however, to me, that Alan Keyes could have become president. It also brings up the possibility that Obama is meant to be the safe candidate for whites. The record may have been 43 and 0, but it is White citizens who are in need of being reassured. Frankly, regular, true black conservatives scare the hell out of whites, on some below the surface, perhaps unconscious level.<br /><br />Negatives:<br /><br />In the middle of a chapter dealing with corruption, Obama writes, \\"So I owe those unions. When their leaders call, I do my best to call them back right away. I don't feel this is corrupting in any way; I don't mind feeling obligated toward home health-care workers who clean bedpans every day for little more than the minimum wage or toward teachers. . .\\" It's not corruption, as long as it's for a good cause. Incredible. I nearly choked on the pretzel I was munching. It's so incredible, this may be the reaction, or the 'awareness' Obama was trying to raise.<br /><br />He's no friend to big business. Corporate America is faltering and could use help. Ronald Reagan put a quota on the number of foreign cars sold in America. There are reasons Obama couldn't do this, one of which might be he doesn't know Reagan did so. There were reasons Reagan couldn't do it either but he did anyway. If I want to work at a dangerous job-site with low pay and benefits, and participate in waste-dumping, I'll work for a small company. Obama is making sure that happens?<br /><br />Obama favors the thought of 'unions'. Both left and right seem to have forgotten: at one point in our nation we simply passed labor laws. The left underestimate their true power? That day could come again. I've admitted I'm a member of the rabid right, but in truth, I could go along with at least half of the leftist agenda, but the other half, that I hold dear, well, that's why I usually end up favoring politicians on the right. For all Obama's change, and his ability to compromise, the changes all seem to be about a grab toward the middle, and this book is about taking a position in the center? Where ever it is Obama comes from, there's the possibility he's trapped, and all politicians are trapped, in some kind of safe-zone, careful area or even within the walls of the 'monied interests' that support them. I would like to see some real compromise, on both sides.<br /><br />Obama writes about the latest Republican ideas and their promotion of an 'ownership society'. I thought perhaps on this one issue Obama might not have fully understood the republicans position here (and who could blame him)? If one looked at John McCain's health care proposal during the 2008 election, McCain was calling for an end to employment based health care. McCain wasn't directly proposing this but it's what his legislation (if enacted) would have led to. In the world I live in, companies have to relocate their offices ever decade or so. Many office employees (most likely married with children) take another job rather than relocating.<br /><br />In one place he writes, \\"when our collective interest in building an upscale new mall is used to justify the destruction of someones home - we depend upon the strength of countervailing values\\". Sounds good, but I doubt Obama has prevented anyone's home from being demolished. 'Eminent domain' is an issue that's been raised by the political right (whether due to principle, or focus group evaluation). Only government can seize property, which they do, when a road needs widened or a new convention center is needed. Obama has the home destroyer's building 'upscale malls'.<br /><br />At times it's the same old narrative. He writes about riots in the country of Indonesia in 1997 but doesn't reveal these were race riots with natives driving out and even slaughtering immigrant Chinese. Instead Obama places the blame on authority, and the figure of authority: the government troops sent out to quell violence. Obama says the Earth is warming. And that would be a bad thing. I was half expecting him to mention Western man's eradication of others with Small Pox, because he knows the idea, 'Western medicine eradicated Small Pox' could give people the wrong idea, and be insensitive. Obama uses the phrase 'the Monroe Doctrine' 3 times - sure enough, it's in the index 3 times - and twice actually talks about how the document was used by the USA to justify imperialist actions - this is the document that was America's, and the worlds, statement against colonialism. American servicemen died enforcing the Monroe doctrine, in some cases outside the Western Hemisphere.<br /><br />I don't want to mischaracterize this book. . . I genuinely recommend it for it's impressive and fair history and civics lessons. . . I say Obama is a reaction to Reagan. . . I also suspect Obama is the left's Reagan. Certainly the economic situate parallels what it was during Reagan's election/presidency. So far Obama is doing a fair job of guiding our country back from a bad situation. Some reviewers notice that - the Obama who wrote this book is not the Obama we have as president. If I was on the left I might wonder if Obama is some kind of closet conservative. Not too much really changes, and in his book, he does a fair job of explaining the conservative position. Not sure what's going on. . . perhaps we all have to wait till 'the nation's third black president'. Perhaps, the less Obama actually says and does, and the more the country turns around, it will all seem due to the magic of Obama. And Obama and the left will be the gate keepers of what is right and wrong. If Obama is the new-Reagan, things aren't looking good for those of us on the right for awhile. One difference is, this time school teachers and the media wont be opposing Obama. In our democracy we need to learn to live with what we have, I suppose.<br /><br />In his chapter on Race he writes, \\"White guilt has largely exhausted itself\\" and then proceeds to mine this vein himself, he writes about the shameful period where the US held Japanese Americans in internment camps. This chapter deserves it's own review but let me just stick with; if we must apologize for this shameful act, why not apologize to the German and Italian Americans held in internment camps also? You can't very well write a book dedicated to minority disadvantage and then have a typical minority portrayed within as a God. In a conversation with Obama, Senator Robert Byrd of West Virginia tells Obama, \\"Gods grace. Yes indeed. Let me sign these for you then\\".<br /><br />I'm not sure what's going on in the minds of liberal Americans. It almost seems as if it is no longer simply fear, a matter of following the path of least resistance, or preferring to be the safe and winning side - it almost seems as if they are promoting this pro-victim idea, today, because in their minds, it will make legitimate some kind of white-victim mentality later on - at least that is a Fantasy they would prefer believing, when in reality they simply prefer to hurt others over being hurt? 0439023521|5.0|I bought this book a while ago, trying to get my eight grader on to something other than Percy Jackson. A Rick Riordan fan to the core, the kid said, \\"It's good...\\" and shoved the book in a drawer. What he'd told me of the plot--a future North America called Panem where 12 districts (the 13th was destroyed) are punished each year for a past uprising by having two children from each district pitted against each in a continent-wide reality show in a fight to the death. It sounded good but I had a lot in my to be read stack.<br /><br />Fast forward to now. I'm working at a middle school and as I catch snatches of the reading conferences, there is much talk of this series. No matter what book I'm dragging around, the kids' question is always the same: \\"Have you read The Hunger Games?\\" I'm glad to say that next week I'll be able to apply in the affirmative (while scouring the class library for a copy of Catching Fire).<br /><br />This book reminds me of it's title: lean, intensive and even brutal. The writing is sparse but powerful, but the characterization is rich and satisfying. Katniss Everdeen lost her father to the mines. Kat's father, who taught her to hunt, told her which plants to eat and sang so true that even the birds gave pause, was a man respected in district 12. And yet, when Kat's mother slips into depression and the food runs out, no one save the baker's son is willing to help her. At the cost of a black eye and bruised face, Peeta burns two loaves of bread and tosses one to Kat. It is her first gift and she never knows how to repay it.<br /><br />The next gift is Gale, a tall, true boy whose rabbit snares she admires in the forest. He calls her Catnip since she mumbled her name the first time he ever asked. They share knowledge first and then food. Soon, they are a team, shooting a deer together that nets a maimed goat for Kat's little sister Prim, an angelic being loved by all. Gale talks of running away and living in the woods, but they both know they won't, not with so many mouths to feed (Gale has little brothers and neither his or Kat's mother seem to be very useful). Gale's father died in the same explosion as Kat's father did.<br /><br />Kat tries to be calm as the lottery for the Hunger Games approaches. Both she and Gale have had to put their name in extra times to get the monthly tessarae to keep the family from starving to death. A ration of rough grain and a few supplies shouldn't be worth a person's life, but in district 12, that's the reality. Gale's name will be in over forty times. Kat's name is entered twenty. Sweet Prim, who Kath no longer takes hunting because she wanted to heal everything, is the only same one. Kat has made sure that Prim never took tessarae for the family. It's Prim first year to have her name entered, being twelve. Her name will go in twice, when she is thirteen, and once more each year until 18, when she will be safe. Kat's job? To stay alive that long so that she can feed Prim. (Although Gale and Kat have a deal to feed the other's family if one of them are killed in the games).<br /><br />What happens, however, is something that Kat could have never planned for, even in her worst nightmare. The name called is an Everdeen, but it isn't her. It's Prim. Screaming and pushing through the crowd of children who have already parted for her, Kat rushes to the stage to volunteer. She has hardly had time to think about what has happened when the boy tribute is named--Peeta, the boy with the bread, the boy who saved her from starving.<br /><br />This is the conflict that keeps the book going, that and Kat's character. Peeta says that she doesn't know what she does to people and he's right. Just like the boy with the bread, we are in love with Kat and feel for Peeta. Book One chronicles televised weeks in the arena that will bring Kat and Peeta face-to-face with their true selves. What will they be willing to do to stay alive?<br /><br />Recommended for school libraries. Readers who like UGLIES by Scott Westerfield or THE GIVER by Lois Lowry will like enjoy this.y will like enjoy this.

B004EQAVHI|4.0|Like the 1985 Terry Gilliam film Brazil, Mark Romanek's Never Let Me Go borrows its title from a sickly sweet nostalgic song. And, like Gilliam's Brazil, Romanek's Never Let Me Go is an act of nostalgia itself; Gilliam's film is nostalgic for the 1940's & Romanek's film is nostalgic for the 1970's. Both directors explore/re-visit cultural terrain that is familiar to them & that they are fond of, but they also use the time period that they are nostalgic for to track the beginnings of current trends.<br /><br />Both films invite us to think about a world that is becoming more & more controlled by an overly bureaucratized state & an omnipresent commercial media & for both filmmakers the result of this state of things is that life feels less & less authentic & less & less real.<br /><br />But there the comparisons end & each film explores these themes in highly distinctive ways. Brazil belongs to a tradition that include both the Kafka-esque & the Monty Python-esque. Never Let Me Go belongs to a slightly older tradition that includes the Victorian novel & theatre (both of which explore the psychic affects of an oppressive social order--an order where a working class majority serves an aristocratic minority).<br /><br />Brazil is a manic & quirky cult classic; Never Let Me Go, with its young stars & lush cinematography, is much more aesthetically pleasing (as many period films are) & in many ways more conventional than Brazil, but its fascinating to view these two films that deal with such similar content in such unique ways together.

0307387178|4.0|An interesting book, about an interesting young man, born 150 or so years after his time. One can easily picture Chris McCandless as one of the men on Lewis and Clark's expedition in search of an overland water route. When the party grew near to settled country on their return, they had by then learned to survive and refit from what they found in the wild, and a few of the men turned right around before they reached the city to join other expeditions headed back west. Mainstream life has lost all its appeal to them by then.     It seems clear that this was McCandless' situation - after several years of intermittent 'tramping', it seemed like he developed a false sense of his knowledge and his capabilities, and just decided to 'go for it'. Life back home or even on the rural farm where he intermittently worked had lost its 'belonging' for him.     Krakauer does his typical exhaustive job in retracing McCandless' steps, his origin, and some of the seminal events that shaped his worldview, but also in typical style, a bit more of Krakauer himself leaks into the narrative than seems necessary. Krakauer is a sympathetic author here, as he was in 'Into Thin Air', but at least in that account, he was a first-person participant in those events. It's a good read, but even as Krakauer seeks to understand McCandless' motivations, he seems eager to explore his own. Me too.

0307594777|4.0|Reading this trilogy was great fun, and I'm eternally glad that I waited till all three were published before I started, as I would not have enjoyed waiting for the story to resume! Yeah, it's a bit trashy and titillating, particularly the first book, but the characters are really interesting, and Larsson gets better as he goes. Interesting that the original title (Men Who Hate Women) was so different than the title the English language publisher settled on - themes of misogyny are strong throughout all three books and Larsson clearly wanted it stated up front with his choice of title. It's almost like he finished writing it, re-read it and decided that we might not get it otherwise. Did the English language publisher have more faith in our ability to read between the lines? Or did they just want to sell more books? :-)    There's a certain 'Kill Bill' horribleness to the serial violence that Lizbeth is repeatedly subjected to that gets a bit numbing, and yet her indomitable will to survive and to not be defined by it seems like an expression of Larsson's own faith in the resilience of the human spirit.     Given that one of the central characters of the books is a Swedish writer/journalist and of course the books were written by a Swedish writer/journalist, I'll always be curious how much of Larsson's sense of himself leaked into the characterization.     This was enjoyable pulp fiction, with a couple of redeeming themes, and an unfamiliar cultural setting that made it more interesting. As I began, great fun!

B002ZG980U|4.0|Last night I saw \\"Inception\\" for the first time. I know, I'm a little late to this party, but with everybody else and their brother giving this movie amazing reviews, I figured why rush to see it? What is one more review of what is clearly the greatest movie of the year?    I now feel more than a little let down by Christopher Nolan's latest piece.    Now, don't get me wrong. \\"Inception\\" was good. Very good, in fact. I highly recommend buying it! While no movie can ever hope to live up to the hype that \\"Inception\\" garnered from the public, though, this one left a particularly bad taste in my mouth given the people and press behind it.    Nolan is most recently known for his blockbuster films \\"Batman Begins\\" and \\"The Dark Knight,\\" gritty and over the top comic book films which provided fanboy and casual moviegoers alike with one hell of a fun joyride, and provides the fanboys with the added bonus of years worth of material to bitch about online. And let's not forget the nightmare fodder that was the late Heath Ledger's re-imagined portrayal of the Joker in the latter film. Even before these films made him a household name, Nolan made \\"Memento,\\" a cult film starring Guy Pierce along with Carrie-Anne Moss and Joe Pantoliano, fresh off the first \\"Matrix\\" movie.    \\"Memento\\" has flown under the radar of most movie watchers since its release in 2000, but is loved by many who have seen it for the very simple, clean, and efficient way it tells the story of Leonard, a man searching for his wife's killer, but who has anterograde amnesia and cannot form new memories. The viewer shares in Leonard's confusion and frustration throughout the story, which is told by showing the scenes in reverse order so the first scene of the movie is the last scene of the story, and the last scene of the movie is the very beginning of the tale. It is a mind-bogglingly simple technique which brings what would have been a good-but-boring character piece and makes it engrossing.    This is a director who knows how to make a movie. We also have a cast which includes the likes of Leonardo DiCaprio, Ken Watanabe, Joseph Gordon-Levitt, and Pete Postlethwaite - all very good or great actors in their own rights. The visuals are awesome in the true sense of the word.    \\"Inception\\" is the story of Cobb (DiCaprio), a thief who enters the subconscious dreams of his targets in order to extract their deepest secrets. Cobb is the best extractor in the world. He is hired by powerful businessman Saito (Watanabe), a previous mark of his, to go into the mind of a rival, Robert Fischer (Cillian Murphy) and plant the concept in his subconscious to dissolve the rival company. Cobb sees it as his last big go at it and his chance to return home to the children he has not seen in years and has only heard the voices of on the phone. The difference is that while extraction of a secret is routine for Cobb, the implanting of an idea, inception, is thought to be impossible, but Cobb and his team are good enough to do it.    How awesome is that?!    The problem is that if you fully divorce the movie from the special effects, it is a fascinating and intriguing plot idea, but ultimately a weak story in execution. The quick cuts, the pacing, and the visuals were all expertly done and did a good job of distracting from that and made the movie, as I said, very very good, but Cobb's kids are Macguffins in the most exploitative sense and leave you with the feeling that they are included simply because...uh... None of the characters are developed beyond one-dimensional charactures, and this is despite a painful amount of effort to force DiCaprio's character into a developmental arc with the kids and dead wife.    To add to the weakness of the story, the ending as a nice touch, but as a character, Cobb was so unsympathetic and undeveloped beyond the desire to get back to his kids that...I don't care. I actually cared more about the Fischer, the antagonist and person whose dreams they were invading, more than any of the main characters because they actually develop Fischer as a whole person with personality, wants, likes, and dislikes.    The writing was average for a blockbuster film (which is to say well done but unremarkable) and relied heavily on the visuals to prop it up. Yes, film is a visual medium and so the dialogue and visual SHOULD work hand in hand, but using one as a crutch for the other is a pet peeve of mine. In \\"Memento,\\" both were simple and very well done. \\"Dark Knight,\\" both were heavily complex and layered, but well done. \\"Batman Begins\\" had straight forward dialogue, but the visuals brought the story up to another level (I am purposefully ignoring the annoying training montage). This...this didn't use visuals to raise the story, but to support it.    Even the visuals, I feel, were smacking me upside the head. In a dreamscape, one would expect a little more subtlety than dramatic shifts in gravity and paradoxical staircases. I really feel like Nolan missed some beautiful opportunities to have visual cues which were just slightly off, hitting that subconscious chord that puts the viewer off balance and lets them really feel they are in a dream.    Cobb describes an architect's job in the dream as setting up the general layout and allowing the dreamer's subconscious fill in the details. I can't help but feel that is what Nolan did with this film, and it feels cheap. \\"Lord of the Rings\\" and \\"Avatar\\" proved that there is a massive market for a movie if you want to stretch it out longer than even the 148 minute running time of \\"Inception,\\" but even with that constraint, better use of characterization would have taken this from a very very good blockbuster and elevated it into the arena of very very good movie.    In the end, \\"Inception\\" is a fun, fascinating, beautiful, and ambitious project that renews my faith that Hollywood can indeed make original and creative material. Well worth a watch and a purchase, but not worth the worship at the altar of Nolan.

I purchased the Pocket Pal at the same time I bought another knife. After trying this sharpener on six knives I realize that for me the best results were found when using it on higher quality knives. I have a few cheap knives that were fairly dull. After using this on them their sharpness was only marginally improved. However, when I used this in my Ka-Bar and my SOG Flash II daily carry knife, I found that their edges were brought back to life. Both knives were cutting again like champs. The SOG knife has a partially serrated blade so the conical diamond file provided on the Smith's Pocket Pal was very useful for that.    As for ease of use, I found that this was much easier to handle with smaller knives. With longer and thicker blades the Pocket Pal was hard to hold onto given the small amount of space you have to grab onto it with. There is just enough space for your thumb and index finger. That being said, use this with dry hands on a hard flat surface. Shorter and thinner blades were much easier to sharpen.    The Pocket Pal seems to be built rather well. It seems durable enough to last a few years with heavy use. Given that it was less than $10 I say that this is a bargain. I say every knife owner should give this a try. It doesn't replace my Spyderco sharpener however this goes places my Spyderco cannot. It's perfect for camping and hunting. Because of it's size I was able to throw it into my EDC bag without it adding any noticeable weight or taking up much space at all.

Ms. Chua probably felt it was the \\"Chinese\\" ways that had caused the rebellion- time to move on.  This is not a book with happy ending for the readers, be forewarned.<br /><br />Either Ms. Chua commandeered the \\"Chinese\\" angle to lend credence for her extreme methods or Ms. Chua herself has serious misconceptions about the \\"Chinese ways.\\"  As a third generation Chinese whose parents grew up in Philippines and herself growing up in a western environment and later on marrying a westerner, it's not a great leap to imagine Ms. Chua's piecemeal interpretation of \\"Chinese culture\\" can be tinted by her relative isolation from it.<br /><br />In conclusion, Ms. Chua's book will shed some lights on how ultra-competitive, kill-or-be-kill overachievers whip their next generation to shape.  But as a commentary on modern Chinese vs. Western parenting, this book can't be more inaccurate and misleading.  I feel like a fool handing my money over to Ms. Chua and I'm trying to save you from the same fate.ay without any self-reflection she clearly needs. B000001ES9|4.0|I came to Rush relatively late in my discovery of hard rock majesty as I was in college at the time and some friends of mine had some Rush cassettes that I borrowed on a whim. I almost immediately fell in love with their debut and for years it was my personal favorite. Through the years, `2112 and `A Farewell to Kings' and even `Fly By Night' have surpassed it but I still love `Rush'. It really is the red-headed stepchild of the catalog as it was the most straight forward heavy metal LP the band ever did and the only one not to feature master skins man and lyricist Neil Peart.  That being said, the first record does feature at least two or three songs that remained in the set list to the present day. Led Zeppelin and Black Sabbath seem to be the most prominent influences on much of the music here, naturally. \\"Working Man\\" with its long guitar break which became a showcase for Alex Lifeson and \\"Finding My Way\\" perfectly display Rush heavy rock chops. Geddy Lee's inexperience as a lyricist shows up quite a bit (\\"hey baby it's a quarter to eight\\" from \\"In the Mood\\") but nothing he should be embarrassed about over 35 years later.  \\"What You're Doing\\" is very Sabbathesque in its thundering riffage and brutal delivery.  `Rush' is very heavy for early metal and while it was idiosyncratic is terms of Rush's career it still made a mark and started a remarkable career of progressive rock genius.

Is the environment on even an elite American college campus like Harvard as misogynistic as depicted in the film? B001IBIHQ4|4.0|As I settled into watching The Pacific, I immediately began trying to compare it to Band of Brothers. Ultimately, this method became unfair to the story being told in The Pacific.  While the film was produced largely by the same team that made Band of Brothers, they are two different stories told differently.  Unfortunately, because of the series' promotion, comparisons between the two series are inevitable.  One can't realistically promote a film based on its connections with another film and not expect comparisons.      This film contains three distinctly different stories about US Marines fighting in the Pacific against the Japanese.  These three stories never merge or diverge, they are told separately, focusing on the exploits of three primary characters; Eugene Sledge, John Basilone, and Robert Leckie.  The director chose to jump around and attempt to tell these stories chronologically and simultaneously. This method can be confusing, disjointing, and disruptive, and it was.  On the other hand, I thought they did a pretty good job of keeping it all together, except that Leckie is pretty much out of the story at the end of the 6th episode, though he comes back for about 15 minutes in the final episode.      As far as watching a war film about US Marines and WWII in the Pacific, this is probably the best film, overall, I've ever seen.  It has enough variety and action to satisfy most any war movie enthusiast.  It has plenty of Spielberg quality combat action, complete with flying body parts, exploding bodies, horrific imagery, cruelty, and fear.  It is a war story of epic proportion told on a grand scale.      I found the film lost tempo when it moved into scenes about the rear.  Covering the rear is all well and good, but I thought the producers spent far too much time in Australia chasing lasses and falling in love at Camp Pentleton than necessary.  Having said that, John Basilone's romance with Lena was probably an integral part to the story, whereas, Robert Leckie's love fling in Australia could have easily been ignored.  Leckie's brief romance just didn't factor into the over-all story, unless of course one wants to make a claim for some kind of esoteric message being extrapolated toward the final episode.      One of the strengths of The Pacific is in the supporting cast.  The actors, both lead and supporting did a fine job, but some of the supporting actors were exceptional.  One that comes to mind is Rami Malek who portrays Merriell \\"Snafu\\" Shelton, a Marine from New Orleans.  Shelton comes into Sledge's unit shortly after Sledge's arrival.  He is a gruesome character who digs gold teeth out of dead Japanese soldiers and behaves immorally in situations that call for candor and dignity.  Nonetheless, he bonds with Sledge, and they help each other get through the war.  Malek's portrayal of Shelton is on the edge and quite good.  We might see more from him in the future.     The Pacific is not a masterpiece, but it is epic and worth watching, and I'm glad I watched it.
